Shen Bao Medicine Weekly

Overview of Domestic Diseases and Epidemic Prevention Measures in the 23rd Year of the Republic

— Shen Bao Yearbook Society

(A) Plague... This year, only Fujian Province reported plague cases nationwide; however, the number was small, with 16 cases found in Suxitou, and 11 cases found in Tieshiyang Village near Longyan City. Although there were reports of plague in the province again until November, the results were inconclusive as they were not verified and confirmed by bacteriologists.

(B) Cholera... Cholera was rarely found throughout the country this year. Only six cases have been verified, the first one found in Guangzhou on June 9th, two found in Hankou on July 4th, and the remaining three found in Shanghai on July 19th and 28th, and all were chronic and soon cured. In addition, suspected cholera patients were scattered in cities and towns in the upper reaches of the Yangtze River and Qingdao. Major cities still held large-scale vaccination campaigns as in previous years. In Shanghai, the Shanghai Municipal Government, the concession health authorities, and the railway health office worked together to carry out vaccinations. By the end of August, a total of 749,625 people had been vaccinated. At the same time, the water company also provided drinking water to the poor, and the health authorities sent personnel to various well locations to carry out disinfection work.

(C) Kala-azar... In China, according to past survey reports, Kala-azar is often found in all of Shandong and Hebei, most of Shanxi, and eastern Henan and Shaanxi, as well as northern Jiangsu and Anhui. In June of this year, the Health Experiment Department of the National Economic Commission established a Kala-azar research team in Qingjiangpu, Jiangsu, to study the treatment and prevention of Kala-azar. According to reports, the team conducted surveys in ten villages from August to December using very precise methods. Among 982 people, 242 suffered from Kala-azar, accounting for 24% of the total population. The team also established hospitals and clinics in the area. As of the end of this year, a total of 201 patients had been hospitalized and 691 new patients had been treated as outpatients.

(D) Leprosy... In recent years, the Chinese people have gradually paid attention to leprosy patients, which can be proved by the enthusiastic establishment of leprosy hospitals and clinics by the government and private organizations this year. Among them, the Chinese Leprosy Association's construction of a leprosy hospital in Wusong, Shanghai with a huge sum of 100,000 yuan is particularly eye-catching. According to reliable estimates, there are a total of 3 million leprosy patients in the world, and China accounts for one-third—one million; however, as far as the reporter knows, there are currently only 23 leprosy hospitals, 17 clinics, and 9 leprosy villages in China, with only about 5,000 people receiving treatment. Therefore, the urgent need to solve the leprosy problem in our country is an urgent matter.

(E) Tuberculosis... How was the situation of tuberculosis in our country in the past? So far, no detailed statistics have been seen, so there is no way to know the real situation. However, according to the results of surveys in Hong Kong, Beiping, and Shanghai, it is known that the number of Chinese people suffering from tuberculosis is not small. The Shanghai China Anti-Tuberculosis Association was established in 1933 and is currently conducting ongoing investigations into tuberculosis patients, and is widely publicizing the importance of the disease to attract the attention of the Chinese people. Not long ago, the association held a conference in Shanghai, bringing together experts from all over the country to jointly discuss various issues related to tuberculosis, and the results were quite good. In Beiping, significant progress has also been made in tuberculosis prevention work recently. There are currently eight tuberculosis institutions with 296 beds. In 1929, there were only two such institutions with five beds. In addition, the Beiping Tuberculosis Hospital is currently being planned and constructed. Its main purpose is to train specialized personnel in tuberculosis prevention and to organize a sanatorium and a tuberculosis public health research institute.

(F) Smallpox and Diphtheria... The importance of preventing and treating smallpox has been generally recognized. Therefore, local health administrative agencies attach importance to vaccination work every year. During the period from July 1933 to June 1934, the Shanghai Municipal Health Bureau alone carried out 246,063 smallpox vaccinations. The attention paid to smallpox by the Chinese people can be seen from this. As for diphtheria, the health authorities of cities such as Beiping and Nanjing also carried out large-scale prevention campaigns this year. According to statistics, the number of vaccinations in Beiping was 4,905, and in Nanjing was 9,038.

(G) Dysentery, Malaria, and Beriberi... According to surveys conducted by the Health Experiment Department in hospitals in Nanchang, Linchuan, Nanzheng, Nanfeng, Guangchang, Baishui, Yiqian, Fengcheng, Changshu, Ji'an, and Taihe in November and December of this year, these three diseases are very severe. Among hospitalized patients, those suffering from dysentery account for about 50%, and malaria and beriberi account for about 25%.

(H) Schistosomiasis... Regarding the work of preventing and controlling schistosomiasis, the Zhejiang Provincial Government has jointly conducted research with the Health Experiment Department, and has established a task force for the prevention and treatment of Japanese schistosomiasis in Qu County and Hangzhou, engaged in the study of the life history of snails and the treatment of patients. According to research reports, it has been proven that water buffalo and dogs are the vectors of this disease. The Hangzhou team has also established two sub-teams: (1) a task force for the prevention and control of fasciolopsiasis, stationed in Xiaoshan, in cooperation with the Xiaoshan Xianghu Normal School in Zhejiang, in addition to medical work, it also engages in various research work. (2) A task force for the prevention and control of pulmonary paragonimiasis, stationed in Shaoxing, studying the life history of pulmonary paragonimus and examining snails, crabs, livestock, and wild animals.

Skin Hygiene and Prevention of Skin Diseases

— Xie Junshou

As the saying goes, "Prevention is better than cure." Therefore, instead of having the best methods to treat diseases, it is better to understand preventive measures to prevent diseases from occurring. This is more time-saving and convenient. Paying attention to skin hygiene can not only prevent skin diseases, but also have a beneficial effect on the internal organs of the whole body, because the relationship between the two is very close. Originally, the skin wraps our entire body and comes into contact with everything in the outside world, like the first line of defense in wartime, resisting all external stimuli and protecting the peace of the internal organs. At the same time, the stronger the skin, the more it can promote the activity of the internal organs. This is the relationship between the skin and the internal organs. However, the health of the internal organs is also related to the skin. For example, when a certain organ has a disease, many changes can occur on the skin. For example, people with heart disease and lung disease often have a haggard appearance. The luster and color of their skin are also not radiant, but completely pale. Also, the complexion of people with insomnia and neurasthenia is often not healthy. The skin of people with jaundice is yellow, and the skin of people with cancer is earthy. When we see changes on the skin, we can infer what kind of changes are happening in the internal organs. Therefore, to prevent skin diseases, in addition to studying ways to strengthen the skin itself, we must also pay attention to the health of the whole body in order to achieve the real purpose of prevention.

What is skin hygiene? In practice, it aims to maintain and enhance the health of the skin. That is, strong skin is resistant to external stimuli. Even if a disease invades, it can resist it, or even if it cannot be completely avoided, it will not become a serious illness. This is the primary meaning of prevention. However, when it comes to skin hygiene, it is also related to beauty techniques, because most skin diseases are detrimental to beauty. The purpose of most patients seeking medical treatment is also to worry about the damage to their beauty. Therefore, treating skin diseases not only relieves the patient's pain, but also pays attention to the beauty aspect.

Speaking of the meaning of beauty, from our medical common sense, the appearance wrapped in diseased skin is not beautiful and is ugly. If we can promote the health of the skin, we can naturally maintain its beauty. That is, paying attention to skin hygiene can promote its beauty. In other words, true beauty can only be found in healthy skin. Therefore, to protect the beauty of the skin, it is essential to pay attention to skin hygiene.

According to what has been said above, skin hygiene, in addition to paying attention to the nutrition of the whole body, also requires attention to strengthening the skin. Strong skin can not only enhance the skin's resistance to external stimuli, but also enable the skin's physiological functions to develop fully, giving weak skin vigorous vitality. The way to strengthen the skin is to train it with air and sunlight being the most necessary. Direct contact with the cold and warmth is important to train a habit of resistance. This is even more important in childhood where the effects are more significant. For example, when going out, wrapping children heavily with gloves and scarves, wearing thick leather clothing, and using hats and umbrellas to block the sunlight are all inappropriate because meaningless fear of cold and small breezes will only make the skin weaker. A slight carelessness will lead to colds. Therefore, such habits should be strictly prohibited.

Exposure to fresh air is also one of the important conditions for hygiene. Regardless of winter or summer, whenever the weather is good, it is best to walk in the suburbs, forests, or countryside. This can make metabolism active and blood circulation vigorous, resulting in a beneficial effect on the skin. Outdoor exercise is extremely beneficial to the skin, especially bathing in sunlight, which has even greater effects. This is because the temperature of sunlight, acting on the skin, not only enhances the skin's function, but also the important ultraviolet rays in sunlight are absorbed into the human body, causing vitamins to be produced in the blood (this is called anti-rickets nutrients), making metabolism obviously vigorous. At the same time, direct stimulation of the skin invigorates physiological functions, not only increasing the skin's resistance, but also, due to the great bactericidal power of light, killing various bacteria attached to the skin. When we treat patients with artificial sun lamps, even for a short time, patients often feel refreshed, breathe smoothly, have a full pulse, feel physically energized, and full of vitality. The skin of farmers in the countryside and outdoor workers is mostly strong, not because they were born that way, but because they are accustomed to strong winds and blazing sun from childhood, bathing in sunlight. It is the result of long-term training. If someone wants to quickly change their lifestyle, do strenuous exercise, or give themselves drastic stimulation, it would be dangerous. Being able to gradually pay attention to training and practice, once accustomed, one can naturally obtain healthy skin.

Cold water bath and cold water friction are good ways to train the skin. Cold water bath is very intense and only suitable for adults. It is mostly unsuitable for the elderly, children and women. In winter, more attention should be paid as there is a risk of catching a cold. People who usually use cold water bath often feel some shivering in the bath. Therefore, when entering the bath, it should be stopped when the hair stands on end and immediately get out of the bath, using a dry towel to rub vigorously until the whole body is warm. If it is too cold, adding some hot water is okay.

Cold water friction is easier to implement than cold water bath. It involves using a handkerchief or towel soaked in cold water to rub the skin of the whole body, starting from the shoulders and reaching the chest, back, abdomen, both wrists, buttocks, etc. Rapidly rub the skin. The timing of implementation is the same as for cold water bath. It is best to do it when getting up in the morning, or it can also be done before going to bed. Both of these methods stimulate the skin

Page 16

nerves. Appropriate implementation can make blood circulation vigorous and the skin strong, which is very beneficial for the health of the whole body.

Dry friction is the safest method, and it is easy for women and children to implement. Use a dry towel to rub the skin of the whole body. The order of friction is the same as for cold water friction, starting from the shoulders and reaching the feet. Or, instead of using a towel, one can quietly rub the skin with their own palms. Some people with sensitive skin often find it difficult to implement cold water friction or dry friction. One can first rub with the palms, and after getting used to it, then use cold water friction.

Seawater bath can also be regarded as a kind of cold water friction. However, the saltiness contained in seawater has no special significance for the skin. Sometimes, it often worsens certain skin diseases, such as eczema, prurigo, etc., when exposed to sea tides and wind. However, bathing in the sea, due to the swaying of the waves, stimulates the nerves of the skin, making physiological functions vigorous. At the same time, lying naked in the sand on the beach for a sunbath is truly killing two birds with one stone. It is an excellent opportunity for skin training.

Warm water bath can dilate the blood vessels of the skin and promote sweat secretion, which can promote metabolism. In a short period of time, it can make the mind refreshed and is very important for hygiene. Especially when taking a warm bath, using a brush or sponge to remove dirt and rub the skin of the whole body can help nourish the skin. However, to train the skin and prevent colds, cold water and dry friction are relatively better. Hot water bath can cleanse the skin, which is also very important for hygiene.

In short, people with sensitive skin are prone to skin diseases and colds. Often use the above methods to train the skin, continuing to implement it between winter and summer. Weak skin can become strong, and a considerable preventive effect can be obtained for colds and skin diseases. (To be continued)

Communication in Chinese and Polish Medical Circles

— Zhang Yinming

Two Polish Doctors Visit China to Take Office in the Examination Yuan

Professor Weigl Invents Typhoid Fever Vaccine

Polish medicine also enjoys a considerable reputation in the world. For example, the University of Krakow and the University of Lviv have made significant contributions to medical research. However, there has been little historical connection between the Chinese and Polish medical communities. In June 1933, two famous young Polish doctors, Weinert and Willeski, were enthusiastic about the study of medical psychology, especially various problems in psychopathology. They came to Asia for field study, passing through Romania, Soviet Russia, Turkey, Persia, India, Penang, Singapore, and Hong Kong to reach Shanghai. They contacted the Chinese Medical Association and served at the Suzhou Mental Hospital for one month. They then joined the Sino-Polish Cultural Association. In 1934, the Examination Yuan hired Weinert as the director of the Health Department and Willeski as a health consultant. Both were able to fulfill their duties. Willeski traveled north for three months, working in the neurology department of the Peking Union Medical College. Weinert accompanied President Dai Jitao and the Panchen Lama to Hangzhou last summer to preside over various health affairs. The two left China for Japan on December 17 last year and then went to the Americas. They are scheduled to return to Poland in June of this year to complete their world tour and field research feat. The two have lived in China for more than a year and a half, and their medical skills have won the trust of all parties. The relationship between the Chinese and Polish medical communities has become increasingly close. There are many Polish priests who have stayed in China as missionaries, especially in North China, and many have established hospitals for medical treatment. The most famous is the Polish Catholic Eye Hospital in Shunde, Hebei. According to precise statistics, 75% of Polish missionaries who came to China died of typhoid fever. In 1930, Professor Weigl, director of the Biology Department of the University of Lviv in Poland, invented the most effective typhoid fever vaccine. After years of experimentation and immersed in practical work, he finally achieved success. The following year, Father Luton brought the Weigl typhoid fever vaccine to China and conducted preventive injections in various Catholic churches with remarkable results. The Weigl typhoid fever vaccine was initially manufactured only in Professor Weigl's laboratory. In 1931, the Belgian Catholic Church in Beiping requested Professor Weigl in Poland to disclose the formula for local manufacturing due to satisfactory trial results. The professor replied with a telegram of approval. The church then sent Dr. Zhang Hanmin to Poland to study with the professor, staying in Poland for two months and obtaining the manufacturing method before returning. Therefore, the Belgian Catholic Church in Beiping was also able to manufacture this vaccine thereafter. The mortality rate of Polish priests was sharply reduced after this preventive injection. The Weigl typhoid fever vaccine is only prevalent among various Catholic churches in China and has not been widely used. It is hoped that the Chinese medical community can give it due research. At the National Pharmaceutical Conference held in the capital from October 3 last year, Doctors Zhang Hanmin and Luo Zhong mentioned Professor Weigl's new invention, which aroused considerable attention from the entire audience. The Pope awarded Professor Weigl the honorary title of Bishop of St. Gregory in recognition of his meritorious invention.

Common Sense Questions and Answers About Diseases

— Cheng Hanzhang

(54) Beriberi

(Q) What is Beriberi?

(A) Beriberi is a systemic nutritional disorder, a disease caused by a lack of Vitamin B1 in the diet.

(Q) Where does Beriberi occur most frequently?

(A) Mostly in places where white rice is the main food, like parts of East Asia, South America, and Africa.

(Q) Is the lack of Vitamin B1 in food the sole cause of Beriberi?

(A) Most scholars have decided that the cause of Beriberi is the lack of Vitamin B1 (Thiamine) in food. However, true Beriberi may not be exactly the same as Vitamin B1 deficiency in humans. However, except for the heart symptoms, the other symptoms are very similar.

(Q) What is the difference in heart symptoms between Beriberi and Vitamin B1 deficiency in humans?

(A) Heart symptoms are very significant in Beriberi; while circulatory symptoms are not very obvious in Vitamin B1 deficiency in humans. However, digestive disorders are very severe.

(Q) What theories are there today to explain the cause of Beriberi?

(A) There are two theories: poisoning and infection.

(Q) What is the poisoning theory?

(A) The poisoning theory is white rice poisoning. This theory is more powerful, because Beriberi is only seen in populations that eat white rice. Also, bird white rice disease is similar to Beriberi, because white rice has had the important vitamins in the bran removed.

(Q) What is the infection theory?

(A) The starting point of the infection theory is that Beriberi is epidemic and related to the seasons of the year. However, fewer people believe this theory.

(Q) What are the predisposing factors for Beriberi?

(A) There are many predisposing factors: First, warmth and humidity, so it is more common in the hot and humid summer, followed by spring and autumn, and less common in winter. Second, excessive exercise or lack of exercise. Third, mental fatigue. Fourth, various infectious diseases such as typhoid fever, influenza, tuberculosis, kidney disease, anemia, diabetes, wasting diseases, and pregnancy and childbirth.

(Q) Is there a relationship between Beriberi and constitution?

(A) There is no certainty as to whether there is a constitution that is prone to Beriberi. However, Beriberi is more common in people with acquired nervousness or thymolymphatic constitution.

(Q) Is there a relationship between Beriberi and age and gender?

(A) More common in adult males, most common from 15 to 25 years old, and less common in young people and the elderly.

(Q) Is there a relationship in terms of occupation?

(A) Apart from farmers, it is most common among people in the lower classes. It also occurs frequently in prisoners.

It is most common among fishermen, sailors, miners, female textile workers, monks, and nuns.

Common Sense of Ear, Nose, Throat and Eye Hygiene

— Tang Renjin

(37) The Reason Why the Eye Can See (Continued)

Emmetropia

Because the retina is covered with optic nerves, which can cause vision. Also, if the distance between the condensing lens and the film in a camera can be arbitrarily stretched (telescopic bellows), the image can be made clearer. The same effect also occurs in the eyeball, because the crystalline lens can change its curvature at will according to the distance of the object through the action of the ciliary muscle (Figure 11), thereby making the image clearly projected onto the retina. This action is the adjustment action of the eyeball.

By observing the structure and physiological function of the eye mentioned above, we can naturally understand the reason why the eye can see. Therefore, my conclusion is... When the light of an object enters the cornea of our eyeball, it is refracted by the crystalline lens and transmitted to the visual center of the brain through the optic nerve of the retina to produce vision. Furthermore, the adjustment of the eyeball makes this image particularly clear.

The establishment of emmetropia is achieved by the implementation of the functions possessed by the camera, as described in the previous section "The Reason Why the Eye Can See." This emmetropic eye is an eyeball with ordinary and sound vision. Its ability can adjust the iris, refract the crystalline lens, and use its natural adjustment to completely project the image produced by light, whether far or near, onto the retina (refer to the attached diagram). Therefore, objects near and far can be seen clearly and are not subject to any restrictions. Also, there is no need for the assistance of corrective glasses. The ability of this eyeball is completely in line with the function of a camera. Personnel who wish to serve in the army, aviation, customs, and pilotage, etc., can always pass the eyesight test without any problem if they have this type of emmetropic eye.

Diagram of Retinal Photosensitivity of Emmetropic Eye

Medical Pain Relief (4)

— Li Xing

Beneficial and Harmless Pain Relief Techniques

(1) Rest: Generally speaking, pain requires rest. No matter what kind of pain, it is necessary to rest when the pain is aggravated by movement. For example, if a red eye hurts severely, resting the eye is necessary in addition to applying eye drops. The same is true for pain in other parts of the body. When any part of the body is inflamed or injured and painful, it should be rested. Resting an inflamed part can, one, reduce pain, and two, help the body's repair work to recover its original state quickly. This method is very common and everyone can do it. It seems that there is no value in describing it, but it is not. Although it is very common, it is very important in the method of pain relief. Special attention should be paid.

(2) Lying Position: The patient should rest in bed in a certain lying position that makes him feel less pain. If there is such a lying position, help him achieve it. For example, sometimes it is necessary to raise the swollen part of the body to make blood flow back easily, which can reduce pain. Here is an example. I used to have a patient with heart disease. He was fine in the hospital. Later, he went home. After returning home, his illness worsened again, and he returned to the hospital. I saw that his legs were very swollen and he felt bloated and painful. I immediately asked him to lie on the bed and raised the bed at his feet and legs, so that his lower limbs were raised about 80 degrees. In less than half an hour, the swelling of his legs completely disappeared, and he no longer felt pain. From this, it can be seen that a suitable posture is also of considerable value. Suitable postures must also be changed from time to time. If a lying position is maintained for too long, it is easy to make the limbs inflexible.

(3) Activity: Rest is of course the most important thing, but activity should also begin at the appropriate time. For example, when a bone is broken, after the bone is set, it must be kept completely still for a month or two to allow the fracture to heal completely, but when it does not hinder the broken bone, it should be moved. The same is true for all inflamed parts. Once it is about to heal, it should be moved. In this way, the initial activity may inevitably be slightly painful, but continued activity will show progress. When starting to move, the force must be gradually increased from light to heavy, and the time must be gradually increased from short to long. Do not overdo it to avoid injury to the inflamed area. (To be continued)

A Brief Discussion of Pulmonary Tuberculosis (43)

— Hu Jiayan

Chapter 9 Clinical Course

Now we can mention the complete types, the general situation of exudative and proliferative! Attached to these two, there are also several sub-items of distinction; this has been mentioned in the chapter on pathological anatomy. Regarding the characteristics of each of their types, it would of course be very tedious to describe them one by one; here, it is better to single out two significant types, caseous pneumonia and acute miliary tuberculosis; because they are relatively common in clinical practice, and one represents exudative and the other represents proliferative. The detailed course of both sides is now described separately below:

(1) Caseous Pneumonia This is the most serious type. It is acute and sudden in onset. At the beginning, it develops a high fever, which can reach 39 or 40 degrees Celsius. This, on the surface, is very similar to typhoid fever, sepsis, pyemia, and other symptoms in acute infectious diseases. It is especially similar to ordinary acute pneumonia, but the initial state of shivering is less common! Dullness appears in the lungs, and bronchial breathing sounds appear during auscultation. These are very significant lung changes, and the state of dissolution is not very visible. High fever remains and does not subside, forming a remittent fever type. The sputum is purulent at the beginning, sometimes appearing green, and rusty color is rarely seen; there are many tuberculosis bacteria and elastic fibers in the sputum, which is the only proof and the difference from ordinary pneumonia. But at the beginning, sometimes it is not seen in the specimen. Protein sometimes appears in the urine, the spleen is enlarged, the blood image shows a serious toxic state, the white blood cell count increases, polynuclear cells increase, lymphocytes decrease, and the blood sedimentation rate also increases. The trend of caseous pneumonia is mostly from an acute and serious state, falling into incurable within a few weeks. If this acute difficult point is passed, the temperature is lower at this time, and other general conditions will also be slightly better; but the discovery of cavities is inevitable, and the result becomes chronic cavitary disease! This, in the final result, is also mostly bad. In terms of diagnosis, it is relatively difficult to distinguish from pneumonia, lung abscess, and other symptoms. The most important aspect of this is to tirelessly inquire about the medical history and perform a precise examination of the sputum! X-ray examination also has some help. As for its cause, apart from a part that occurs directly in healthy people, most of it is a complication of each stage of pulmonary tuberculosis; in other words, the last step of every type of pulmonary tuberculosis is that many develop caseous pneumonia as a final ending.

Page 17

Economic Supplement

On Rural Investment

— Wei Dong

In recent years, the decline of China's rural economy has drawn widespread attention. Calls for relief have resonated across the nation. Under the leadership of the central government, organizations such as the Rural Rehabilitation Committee of the Executive Yuan and the Agricultural Department of the National Economic Council have been established. Under the jurisdiction of local governments, various rural relief committees have been set up by provincial, municipal, and county governments. In the private sector, charitable organizations have launched disaster relief efforts, and the banking sector has engaged in rural investment. In particular, the advocacy and implementation of rural investment by the banking sector have been more vigorous and rapid than other rural movements. Here, we will examine and discuss several points under the popular topic of "Rural Investment by the Banking Sector."

1. Why Do Banks Invest in Rural Areas?

To understand why banks invest in rural areas, one does not need in-depth research. A simple examination of the dynamics of the banking industry itself will reveal their primary motives. In theory, the establishment of banks is to serve society, concentrating idle funds on the one hand and providing working capital to industry, agriculture, and commerce on the other. However, it is not a charitable organization dedicated solely to helping others. Setting aside theory and looking at the reality, the greatest ambition of most bank operators is simply: (1) to profit from the difference between high lending rates and low deposit rates, and (2) to seek surplus by engaging in various speculative or near-speculative activities using their concentrated financial strength. Chinese banks have always held this view. In recent years, the Chinese economy, under the dual pressure of an increasingly severe global economic crisis and widespread rural collapse across the country, has declined rapidly. Naturally, the banking industry within the Chinese economic sphere has also faced significant harm. Regarding their first ambition, after the bankruptcy of the rural economy, agricultural funds flowing into commercial cities could no longer return to the countryside for agricultural production. Instead, they were deposited in banks to earn interest, leading to a large increase in bank deposits. At the same time, the massive influx of foreign manufactured goods damaged China's fledgling national industries. The decline in the purchasing power of the general population led to the decline of commerce nationwide. As a result, factories and shops closed down one after another, shaking the foundation of bank lending and causing a sharp decrease in the amount of loans issued. The development of these two divergent trends caused the balance between bank deposits and loans to be lost. In 1932, the total deposits of all 146 banks in the country amounted to 2,183,959,957 yuan, while the total loans amounted to 1,946,864,137 yuan (reference: 1934 Banking Yearbook published by the Bank of China). The ratio of loans to deposits was still 89%. However, by 1933, the decrease in this ratio was somewhat alarming. Taking only the operations of the 57 major banks and trust companies generally recognized, total deposits had reached 2,032,014,626 yuan, which was nearly the same as the deposits of all 146 banks in 1932. However, loans in 1933 were only 1,385,537,852 yuan (reference: "Statistics on the Business Reports of Banks and Trust Companies in 1933" by Pan Hengmin, Central Bank Monthly, Vol. 3, No. 10). The ratio of loans to deposits had dropped to 68%. After 1933, China's economic situation not only did not improve but became even worse than before. Judging by the overall trend, the ratio of loans to deposits in 1934 was probably even smaller than in the previous year. Regarding their second ambition, Chinese banks in the past, due to environmental and historical reasons, could not compete with foreign banks in China in the operation of modern financial businesses. For traditional financial activities, money shops and native banks had already divided their spheres of influence, not allowing banks to intervene. As a result, their business gradually drifted into an abnormal state of development, following foreign banks and specializing in various speculative activities. The most important of these were real estate investment and short selling and buying of public bonds. Secondly, they provided loans for various risky ventures, such as cinemas, dog racing tracks, or entertainment venues similar to gambling institutions. Taking Shanghai as an example, tall buildings are everywhere. Looking at their names, they are either a certain bank's building or a certain bank's villa. Various exchanges and margin trading markets not only have daytime trading but some even advocate nighttime trading. As for the development of tea houses, theaters, and other entertainment venues, it coincided exactly with the collapse of the countryside. When the Chinese rural economy first went bankrupt in 1932, this prosperity decorated the major commercial cities very lavishly. However, this prosperity was only a manifestation of the concentration of idle funds without proper productive use. Without a sound rural economy as its foundation, it was like a building built on floating sand, and soon various dangers were discovered, threatening collapse. In addition, foreign aggression occurred. After the Northeast Incident, the Shanghai War followed, and North China and South China were frequently unstable. As a result, the non-standard businesses operated by various banks gradually encountered difficulties. The masses who flowed into commercial cities due to rural bankruptcy could not rent the luxurious buildings built by the banking industry. The decline of domestic Chinese industries, the instability of the situation, and the ever-changing monetary policies of various capitalist countries made it difficult for the banking industry to operate short selling and buying businesses.

Bank operators are the smartest businessmen. They would never allow other people's cash to stay in their own vaults just because the above two ambitions have failed, and still have to pay others a large amount of interest. If their old profit-making methods are ineffective, they can open up new profit routes. Thus, the operation of agricultural lending has attracted their attention. Agricultural loans are mostly secured by agricultural products and land, especially land. Land, in the eyes of bankers wearing gold-rimmed glasses, is the most rigid real estate. In the past, not only did commercial banks not have the idea of "investing in rural areas," but even banks named after agriculture and industry abandoned their responsibilities and preferred to compete with commercial banks in the same business. However, in these extraordinary times, land in agriculture, although it is one of the factors of production like machinery in industry, its stability is much stronger than that of machinery. Because China is a large agricultural country, more than 80% of the people are farmers. The stagnation of industrial production cannot completely eliminate the opportunities for survival of the Chinese people. Agricultural production is an indispensable survival function for the Chinese people. Even if the Chinese economy continues to decline relentlessly, agriculture is still standing on the last stage.

Why do Chinese banks invest in rural areas? Simply put, it is to find a way out for their excessive deposits. If you have to put a charitable mask of "relieving the countryside" on the faces of bankers, you can only say that bankers want to increase the purchasing power of the general public to encourage the prosperity of industry and commerce, and through the prosperity of industry and commerce to develop their lending to industry and commerce. They have to restore the productive capacity of the countryside, so they want to relieve the countryside and invest in the countryside.

2. Technical Issues of Rural Investment

The methods used by the Chinese banking industry for rural investment can be divided into three types: (1) agricultural warehouse loans, (2) farmer mortgage loans, and (3) cooperative loans. The first two methods are directly operated by the banks themselves and do not need to be discussed here. The third method is to use cooperatives as an intermediary between the banking industry and the countryside, which is also the most popular social movement in recent times. Currently, the cooperatives led by the banking industry include production cooperatives, marketing cooperatives, production and marketing cooperatives, and credit cooperatives. Cooperatives are a modern economic organization, the main purpose of which is to avoid exploitation between producers and consumers. In Europe, America, and the Soviet Union, this organization is quite common. However, the emergence of a social movement or social organization is determined by the social environment and needs. For example, the Soviet Union can implement socialism, but the United States currently does not have this need. Furthermore, the representative system that has been popular in politics in recent centuries can maintain its excellent characteristics in European and American countries, but when implemented in our country, many shortcomings of bribery during elections were discovered in the early years of the Republic of China. Although the theory of cooperatives is good, it does not necessarily become popular in China immediately. Even if China can have such cooperative organizations, under the rush movement, it may not be able to reach the true conditions of cooperatives, because the masses participating in this organization should at least reach a certain level of cultural level. To be honest, China is a culturally backward country, and all progress is a deformity. The economic situation is like this, and the education situation is also like this. A small number of people who have received higher education can talk about socialism and Asian production methods, but most of the people are fundamentally illiterate and do not know what economics is. It is absolutely impossible to organize 320,000 cooperatives like the Soviet Union now, because the Soviet Union has now eradicated illiteracy. What about China? At least I am afraid that more than half of the people are illiterate. Perhaps some people will say that all social movements gradually develop. We must know that the bankruptcy of the Chinese rural economy now seems to be a "stroke" of acute illness. Hoping to relieve the countryside through cooperatives is like hoping that children will grow up to study medicine and then treat the disease. The urgency and the slowness are different. If we must quickly popularize the organization of cooperatives, in the author's prejudice, I feel that there are the following difficulties.

(1) Regarding the issue of personnel organizing cooperatives Because of the lack of knowledge of general farmers, all the organization and operation of cooperatives can only completely rely on the leaders engaged in the cooperative movement. Currently, the talents in China who can lead the organization of cooperatives are only the highly educated class who have received cooperative education and the local gentry who have authority in the countryside. Taking the former talents as an example, the highly educated class who have been baptized in the 20th century, read foreign books, and wear foreign clothes may not be able to get used to the rural life that is still in the 18th and 19th centuries. Perhaps there will be jokes like agricultural students in universities being afraid of mud getting their suit pants dirty. At the same time, students who are used to reading foreign books may not be able to understand the situation in the Chinese countryside, and illiterate farmers may not be able to accept Western civilization. The author once heard someone engaged in the agricultural cooperative movement say the following: "The matter of cooperatives is very easy. You get a salary of dozens of dollars a month, and there are also dozens of dollars for office expenses. Office expenses can be used to buy personal things. When you are transferred, all the things bought with office expenses can be brought by individuals, because this is a test nature. As for the work, it is not hard. The people who come to inspect from above will not necessarily ask you in detail. You can just talk nonsense and get by. If someone visits, it doesn’t matter, because they don’t even understand what cooperation is." This can represent the achievements of cooperative undertakings led by the educated class. Taking the latter talents as an example, the local gentry are the authorities in the countryside, and most of them are landlords above the rich peasants. Although they do not know what cooperation is, they know how to exploit the farmers. The result of the cooperative established under this special force is to change the previous individual oppression of farmers into collective oppression of farmers. The farmers themselves have not been relieved of the pain of being exploited.

(2) Regarding the issue of capital collection for cooperatives Banks lend to the countryside through cooperatives, so before farmers can obtain bank loans, they must first organize cooperatives. The organization of cooperatives requires members to contribute capital. In the current extreme poverty of the rural economy, general farmers will not have surplus capital to be used as cooperative capital. Although the amount of cooperative shares is very small, most tenant farmers and owner-cultivators almost always return agricultural products to general usurers after the harvest season, and then borrow money from usurers to maintain their lives. In this repeated borrowing life, not only is it impossible to participate in cooperatives, but general usurers may not allow them to break away from the bondage of usury. As a result, the so-called agricultural cooperatives are nothing more than exploitative groups organized by a small number of landlords using capital, using the strong power of financial capital to intensify the economic oppression of farmers.

From the above two points of view, it can be determined that China's cooperative undertakings are somewhat different from the cooperative undertakings in Europe and America. Last year, British cooperative credit expert Strickland

Report after inspecting cooperatives in Shaanxi, Hebei, and Hubei provinces: (Ta Kung Pao, December 12, 1934) In the three provinces he inspected, none of the cooperatives had audited their accounts, which shows that the cooperative organization is not sound. Another example is the rural relief movement in Zhejiang last year. There were 42 agricultural financial institutions and as many as 1,072 cooperatives in the province. Everyone thought that the greatest success would be achieved in the end; unexpectedly, hope is hope, and the fact is the fact. According to the report of the Zhejiang Provincial Production Conference, in terms of the stacking situation in Lanxi County, "the limit on the amount of mortgage is very high. It must be mortgaged for 20 yuan or more than 50 yuan. Small and medium farmers certainly do not have such a large output. As a result, 80% to 90% of the mortgagors buy agricultural products at low prices and mortgage them to the stack to wait for the price to sell, and use the mortgage income as funds to buy agricultural products at low prices." This is even more obvious that the cooperative undertakings are manipulated by a small number of wealthy people, and the effect is to intensify the exploitation of farmers.

The author never opposes cooperative undertakings in theory, but in fact, cooperative undertakings need to be promoted by cultural level. As mentioned above, the collapse of the Chinese rural economy is a "stroke" of an acute illness, and the cooperative is only a future doctor. If you call him to complete the ten-year school course that the doctor should study in ten days, the result must be a quack doctor. We also need to know that the decline of the Chinese rural economy now is the production bankruptcy of tenant farmers and small owner-cultivators. These tenant farmers and owner-cultivators account for the absolute majority of Chinese farmers, and the current cooperatives seem to only help rich farmers and large landlords to improve seeds and agricultural mechanization. It is of no benefit to farmers who rely solely on physical strength to survive. Therefore, China's cooperative undertakings only had 3,618 societies in 1931, and increased to 9,948 societies in 1934. Among them, agricultural cooperative societies increased from 3,328 societies to 9,578 societies (National Cooperative Society Statistics), but China's agricultural panic did not abate as a result. This is a very obvious example.

3. The Future of Rural Investment and Land Issues

The collapse of China's rural economy, natural disasters, man-made disasters, and the development of the economic power of imperialist countries in China are all great driving forces, but the uneven distribution of land is also a very important element. Although China does not have the obvious phenomenon of concentrated land like Russia in the Tsarist era, in recent centuries, the trend of land merging from the hands of small farmers to rich farmers, and then from rich farmers to large landlords, has been a generally accepted fact by scholars studying rural economics. The concentration of land, no matter in which political system, will have a very bad impact, because the more concentrated the land, the more poor farmers will increase, the more poor farmers increase, the more farmers will leave the countryside, the more barren the farmland will be, resulting in social disorder and the decline of national agricultural production. (The concentration of land in socialist countries is an exception). Therefore, the core of Sun Yat-sen's theory of the Principle of People's Livelihood advocated in the Three Principles of the People is the equalization of land ownership. The so-called equalization of land ownership is to distribute the land evenly, so that farmers can have their own land. Before the implementation of the equalization of land ownership, the main land policy is to restrict the concentration of land. Now, this rural investment in the financial industry has the effect on agricultural land of being a vital force to promote the accelerated concentration of land. Because if the banking industry directly lends to farmers, of course, for the sake of stability, it will first lend to large landlords who own huge amounts of land. Then the function of the banking industry's investment is to improve the production of Chinese agriculture. The effect of this improvement is naturally to eliminate the farmers' internal labor force by mechanical ability, and the profits obtained from the increase in production become the capital for large landlords to annex small landlords' land. Thus, financial capital becomes the exploitation tool of large landlords after being dyed with agricultural capital. As for the direct operation of farmer mortgage or warehouse loans by banks, it is an investment in farmers below small farmers. According to this investment method, the financial capital that runs into the countryside will eventually directly operate agriculture, applying financial capital to agricultural productivity and gradually turning agricultural land from mortgage into a part of the capital of the financial industry. If cooperatives are used as an intermediary between banks and farmers, there is not much difference from the direct operation of banks, because banks can control the cooperatives they lead, and cooperatives can directly manage farmers. Although there is a separation of cooperatives between farmers and banks, the penetration of economic power is not prevented by intermediate institutions. For example, farmers send agricultural products to cooperatives as collateral and request loans. On the surface, the right to dispose of these mortgaged agricultural products belongs to the cooperatives. In fact, the funds of the cooperatives are the loans of the banking industry. Therefore, the final right to dispose of these mortgaged agricultural products naturally belongs to the banks. Therefore, after farmers' products flow into cooperatives, it is no different from depositing them in bank warehouses. According to the regulations of the cooperatives, these agricultural products used as collateral can be taken back by farmers with money, but in fact, the economic ability of general farmers is only maintained in a cycle of borrowing and lending. As a result, most farmers are unable to redeem the collateral. As a result, farmers also invisibly lose the profits that can be obtained from changes in commodity prices and the compensation that can be obtained from engaging in transportation with labor. Undoubtedly, this rural investment in the banking industry is to use scientific methods to overthrow the old-fashioned usurers, thereby taking their exploitative position. If this continues, the trend of agricultural land concentration under the activities of financial capital will be faster than the annexation of large landlords in the past.

Agriculture and land issues cannot be separated. Taking the United States as an example, the agricultural production methods in the United States apply modern scientific and technological techniques, but during the world economic panic, agricultural panic still occurred. At the same time, his panic was not due to poor seeds or backward production technology, because on the one hand, most farmers could not find bread to fill their hunger, and on the other hand, a large number of agricultural products were poured into the vast ocean. This is based on the uneven distribution of land. If we really want to save China's countryside, it is not by hoping that the term of investment in the countryside by the banking industry should be long, the interest rate should be low, there should be no competition, and by improving seeds and applying mechanical cultivation. At least the government should take the policy of restricting land concentration as a starting point, and implement emergency economic relief measures in a unified way, gradually moving from restoring the productive capacity of general farmers to improving production. In this way, perhaps we can overcome this difficult time of agricultural panic that concerns the life and death of China.

Cooperative Systems of Agricultural Mortgages and Loans in Various Countries

— Chen Honggen

Since the complementary development of modern industry and cities, rural land prices have been increasing daily. Land cultivation has transformed from fragmented plots to collective, large farms, and the means of production have shifted from rudimentary farm tools to sophisticated machinery. The use of land has evolved from extensive management with limited manual labor and fertilizers to intensive farming. All these changes require substantial capital, leaving farmers feeling financially constrained. Borrowing from private lenders entails high interest rates, unsuitable for the low-profit nature of agricultural operations. Seeking loans from banks is also difficult due to the long repayment periods typical of agricultural loans and the unsuitability of agricultural land as collateral (conventional banks are reluctant to accept land as collateral). Under these circumstances, farmers must either find their own solutions or rely on government assistance. The former involves credit cooperatives organized by farmers in various countries, while the latter includes farmer or land banks established by governments. Initially, these two entities operated independently. Cooperatives have close ties to farmers, but often lack sufficient capital during their establishment. Members, seeking loans, are often unable to deposit large sums for circulation, thus failing to solve the farmers' financial problems. Banks possess capital, but are too distant from farmers, finding it difficult to understand their individual and family circumstances, making loans difficult to obtain. Later, cooperation between the two parties emerged, with banks lending large sums to cooperatives, which then distributed the funds according to the needs of their members, thereby overcoming the aforementioned difficulties. Currently, cooperative systems of agricultural mortgages and loans in several European and American countries largely follow this principle. Some cooperatives themselves issue bonds to raise capital. Below is a detailed description of the key aspects of mortgage and loan cooperation systems in various European and American countries, for our reference.

Germany's agricultural credit system was established earliest and is considered the most complete today. Its land mortgage cooperative organization is the Land'Challen (Land Mortgage Cooperative). The earliest was the Silesian Mortgage Cooperative in 1769, followed by similar organizations in other regions. Until the end of the 19th century, these were collectively known as the old-style Land Mortgage Cooperatives. Initially, they were formed by large landowners who needed liquid capital, and membership was mandatory: all aristocrats owning land were required to join, regardless of whether they needed a loan. The bank's loans to its members were funded by its bond issuances. The bonds were secured by the value of the members' mortgaged land, so the amount issued could not exceed the amount loaned. Members seeking loans had to declare their collateral, which was then assessed by the bank before an appropriate amount of bonds was issued. Generally, the loan amount could not exceed 50% of the assessed value of the collateral. Members could then sell these bonds on the market to obtain cash. The interest on the bonds was typically 3% or 4%, depending on fluctuations in the financial market. In practice, the cooperative served only as an intermediary between the lender (i.e., the bond purchaser) and the borrower, assuming no responsibility for the repayment of the principal and interest. If the borrower defaulted, the bondholder could only claim directly from the debtor listed on the bond (i.e., by selling the mortgaged property). This system clearly had several shortcomings, the most significant being: (a) the cooperative did not have close ties with the lenders and borrowers, providing no guarantee for the borrower's repayment obligations, thus failing to fully embody the spirit of cooperation; (b) membership in the cooperative should not be mandatory; and (c) membership in the cooperative should not be limited to aristocrats, and small and medium landowners should also have the same opportunities.

After the 19th century, the cooperatives underwent reforms, abolishing class restrictions for membership and establishing special debt reduction funds. Thus, although the collateral for the bonds issued by the cooperative was still the entire mortgaged property, the principal was paid from the cooperative's debt reduction fund. In other words, the cooperative itself was responsible for repayment. The bonds and their attached interest coupons were bearer instruments, freely transferable or resalable. The amount of a loan to a member could reach two-thirds of the assessed value of the collateral, and had to be used for agricultural production. The maximum loan term was eighty years, with repayments made every six months. The total interest was added to the principal, and then the amount was evenly divided into installments.

Since the Land Mortgage Credit Cooperative was not for profit, it had no surplus to distribute to society, and all earnings were rolled into public debt funds. The cooperative's organization included a board of directors, responsible for issuing bonds and reviewing mortgages; a local bureau managing the funds of cooperatives within its region; and a general assembly, responsible for handling important principles and affairs of the cooperative. There was also a permanent committee, tasked with receiving business reports from the board of directors and auditing accounts.

Before the war, there were twenty-eight new and old-style cooperatives, with a total of 300 million marks in bonds issued by the cooperatives. With such rapid development, centralized control was essential. In 1873, the Central Land Mortgage Credit Cooperative was established in Berlin, with eight cooperatives joining. Its task was to assist the cooperatives in issuing bonds and to guarantee them. It also issued Central Land Bonds (Zentral-Pfandbrief) with the same lending conditions as the cooperatives.

Denmark has two land mortgage cooperative systems: the Credit Cooperative and the Mortgage Loan Cooperative, which are discussed separately below:

(a) Credit Cooperatives (Danlsh)(Kreditkassen): The first established was the Jutland Land Credit Cooperative (1851), followed by gradual increases, now totaling fourteen. Their source of funds is also the issuance of bonds, with bonds issued in batches, each batch worth US$1 million or several million dollars. The issuance period is two or three years. All borrowers receiving bonds from the same batch must be jointly liable. The collateral provided by members is limited to the first mortgage, and the loan amount cannot exceed 60% to 66% of the assessed value of the collateral. The term is fifty or sixty years, with repayment also made in installments. To understand the situation of the members and prevent losses, the cooperative sends people to various places to inquire about the economic situation of the members. Each district also has agents reporting to the cooperative. The government does not heavily restrict the cooperative, but can inspect the cooperative at any time. If it is deemed unsound, the government can take over management or dissolve it.

(b) Mortgage Associations: The purpose of this type of organization is to assist the aforementioned credit cooperatives in accepting second mortgages for lending. The lending procedures are the same as before, but because it involves second mortgages, the evaluation is extremely cautious. The loan amount cannot exceed 75% of the evaluation, and the term is twenty-five to thirty years. There are currently nine mortgage loan cooperatives. To avoid mutual competition and conflict, the Central Mortgage Bank of the Kingdom of Denmark was established in 1906 with a capital of 5,360,000 yuan. Its task is to purchase the bonds issued by the various cooperatives to stabilize their market prices.

The Dutch Land Mortgage Cooperative is affiliated with the Utrecht Central Bank and the Findhoven Mortgage Bank. The former was established in 1898, and its constituent units are local farmers' cooperatives and local credit institutions. In 1929, a total of 723 cooperatives joined. The bank's loans are limited to first mortgages, and as with the systems in other countries, it issues bonds. Members' loans must be introduced by the cooperative. The term is usually ten years, but can be as long as twenty years. At the same time, to facilitate long-term credit lending, a special cooperative bank was established with assets of 924,000 Florins, issuing bonds totaling 3,525,000 Florins, and lending 3,378,000 Florins.

The Findhoven Bank was established in 1896. In 1920, a total of 490 cooperatives joined. Loans do not require collateral if the amount does not exceed 50% of the assessed value of the mortgage. The loan term is forty years, and the interest rate for members' loans is slightly higher than the interest rate on the bonds, thus covering various administrative expenses.

The Belgian mortgage cooperative business is concentrated in The Louvain Central Bank. All situations are similar to those in the Netherlands, so they are omitted.

In Sweden, before 1770, the mortgage cooperative business was operated by the Bank of Sweden. In 1831, Mortgage Associations were organized. There are currently ten, each with a specific scope. Any local resident with net real estate assets of more than 1,125 yuan can become a member of the cooperative. The total value of the assets of all members of each cooperative cannot be less than 1,125,000 yuan. The interest rate for members' loans is typically 6%, plus handling fees, and must be repaid in installments. The source of funds for the cooperative is the issuance of bonds. Its circulation range reached Germany and France at its peak, but is now limited to the country. To prevent mutual competition among the bonds of the various cooperatives, the General Mortgage Bank of Sweden was established in 1861. All bonds are uniformly issued by the aforementioned agency, and the situation has since stabilized.

Since the 19th century in the United States, the value of mortgage loans has increased rapidly, indicating the urgent need for capital among farmers. In 1906, the US government passed the Federal Farm Loan Act. According to this law, a Federal Farm Loan Board and twelve Federal Land Banks were established. Each bank's capital was US$750,000, raised by the bank issuing shares of US$5 each. Under the banks, several National Farm Loan Agociation were established, formed by ten or more farmers. After their establishment, they can apply for loans from the bank. Members seeking loans must use land as collateral, and the purpose is limited to improving agricultural operations. The loan amount cannot exceed 50% of the assessed value of the collateral. The total loan amount of each cooperative must not be less than US$20,000. For every US$100 borrowed by a member, they must subscribe to one share of the Federal Land Bank. Subsequently, the cooperative's loans increased daily, and members' subscriptions to the bank's shares also increased accordingly. As a result, the government's investment was gradually repaid, and the shares of the Land Bank became wholly owned by the cooperatives.

Administratively, the cooperative has its own organization, which determines the conduct of its business; above the cooperative is the bank, and the bank is supervised by the loan office. The cooperative is responsible for evaluating the collateral for its members' loans, and usually collects interest and principal on behalf of the bank. If a member fails to repay the debt, the cooperative must be responsible for repayment.

After describing the mortgage cooperation systems of various countries, we cannot help but think of China's current problems. China certainly needs such an organization. In fact, the most common type of cooperative organization established in various places is the credit cooperative, but due to the lack of funds, very few can exist in a sound manner. In recent years, urban banks have also paid attention to rural investment and lending, which is certainly an optimistic phenomenon. However, to hope that this undertaking can develop properly, at least two things must be noted. First, future rural loans must be strictly limited to productive uses; second, China's current land survey and registration are not yet complete, which is also a major obstacle to the rural lending business using land as collateral. It is believed that at least if these problems are resolved, China's rural mortgage lending business can have full prospects for development.

Securities

Bond Market Brewing Last Week

Last week, from February 25th to March 2nd, the Chinese Securities Exchange saw a mostly sideways trend after settlement. Transactions gradually decreased, showing a calm and uneventful atmosphere. This was because various factors were brewing and had not yet been revealed. The financial support measures from various groups had not yet been implemented. The important meeting convened in Hankou had not yet produced results. The issue of Sino-Japanese cooperation was still in a state of cautious maneuvering, neither close nor distant. The rumor of a central government loan was just a rumor, with no clear signs of reality, making people both suspicious and credulous. The market sentiment remained uncertain. All these factors made investors hesitate, transactions decrease, and market prices stagnate. Last week, only Monday, being the last day before settlement, saw a slight squeeze on ordinary bonds, resulting in a slight increase of several jiao. However, the 23 Customs bond fell by 1.1 yuan. The 961 small triangular bond was suspended on Tuesday for the February futures settlement. The April futures auction on Wednesday was relatively lively, with prices increasing slightly by three to five jiao. The 23 Customs bond remained stable. On Thursday, the important meeting convened in Hankou slightly boosted market sentiment, resulting in a rebound in the afternoon and a return of several jiao. The 23 Customs bond rose by 7.5 jiao. The market was unusually dull on Friday and Saturday, with sideways trading and even fewer transactions. Throughout the week, except for Monday and Tuesday, most days saw sideways trading. Therefore, the resulting market prices, except for April, which was newly launched and incomparable, mostly saw slight increases of one or two to four jiao for March. The 19th Charity Bond, 22 Customs Bond, and 96 saw slight increases. The total turnover was 43.595 million yuan, plus 3,000 yuan in spot transactions. Grain

River Rice Sales Stagnant, Both Wholesale and Retail Quiet

(South Shanghai) Yesterday, sources from various gangs were very scarce, with only about ten or so shops (a total of about 2,000 shi of goods). The sales market was exceptionally sluggish, with most shops dropping prices to seek sales. The overall situation showed a declining trend, with little activity in both wholesale and retail. The price of Japonica rice eventually fell by fractions of a jiao. The evening rice market fell by one or two jiao. Yinbianyuan also saw a declining trend, with a drop of about two chi. Duyangjian had no market.

North and South Goods

Red Rice, Black Melon Seeds, and Land Prices All Rise

Red Rice: Recently, both arrivals and inventories have been lacking, and the market has been very strong. Yesterday, according to a telegram from Shantou, the land price has risen by as much as three yuan due to the thin inventory. The cost of top-quality transparent red rice, including shipping costs, must be 38 or 39 yuan, which has become an unsustainable situation. A survey found that high-quality goods in Shanghai are scarce, with only about 200 pieces of medium-quality goods remaining, priced at around 30 yuan, with the hope of further increases.

Black Melon Seeds: The more abundant Xuzhou goods are said to be out of stock locally, and most have been concentrated in the Shanghai market. Therefore, those who previously purchased directly from Xuzhou have turned to the Shanghai market for procurement. The market has become tight as a result, and land prices are steadily rising, making it especially stable. Quotations have been offered from time to time in recent days. High-quality Xuzhou melon seeds are 9.2 or 9.25 yuan, and ordinary ones are 9 yuan. It is expected that the price will continue to rise. It is predicted that if the sales market is smooth, the price may reach the 10 yuan mark.
